<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="background:#f0f0f0;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Periodic_table_(group_13)" title="Template:Periodic table (group 13)"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Periodic_table_(group_13)" title="Template talk:Periodic table (group 13)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Periodic_table_(group_13)&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>boron group</b> are the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> in <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_table_group" title="Periodic table group">group 13</a></b> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, comprising <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> (B), <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> (Al), <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> (Ga), <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a> (In), <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a> (Tl), and perhaps also the chemically uncharacterized <a href="/wiki/Nihonium" title="Nihonium">nihonium</a> (Nh). The elements in the boron group are characterized by having three electrons in their outer energy levels (valence layers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Kotz.2C_Treichel.2C_R._Townsend_2009_351_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-C._Kotz.2C_Treichel.2C_R._Townsend_2009_351-1">[1]</a></sup> These elements have also been referred to as <b>icosagens</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and <b>triels</b>.</p>
<p>Boron is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> while the rest, with the possible exception of nihonium, are considered <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a>. Boron occurs sparsely, probably because bombardment by the subatomic particles produced from natural radioactivity disrupts its nuclei. Aluminium occurs widely on earth, and indeed is the third <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">most abundant element in the Earth's crust</a> (8.3%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Gallium is found in the earth with an abundance of 13 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>. Indium is the 61st most abundant element in the earth's crust, and thallium is found in moderate amounts throughout the planet. Nihonium is never found in nature and therefore is termed a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_element" title="Synthetic element">synthetic element</a>.</p>
<p>Several group 13 elements have biological roles in the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>. Boron is a trace element in humans and is essential for some plants. Lack of boron can lead to stunted plant growth, while an excess can also cause harm by inhibiting growth. Aluminium has neither a biological role nor significant toxicity and is considered safe. Indium and gallium can stimulate metabolism<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>; gallium is credited with the ability to bind itself to iron proteins. Thallium is highly toxic, interfering with the function of numerous vital enzymes, and has seen use as a <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like other groups, the members of this family show patterns in <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a>, especially in the outermost shells, resulting in trends in chemical behavior:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Z</a></th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">Element</a></th>
<th>No. of electrons per <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">shell</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>5</td>
<td>boron</td>
<td>2, 3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>13</td>
<td>aluminium</td>
<td>2, 8, 3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>31</td>
<td>gallium</td>
<td>2, 8, 18, 3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>49</td>
<td>indium</td>
<td>2, 8, 18, 18, 3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>81</td>
<td>thallium</td>
<td>2, 8, 18, 32, 18, 3</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>113</td>
<td>nihonium</td>
<td>2, 8, 18, 32, 32, 18, 3 (predicted)</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The boron group is notable for trends in the electron configuration, as shown above, and in some of its elements' characteristics. Boron differs from the other group members in its <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a>, refractivity and reluctance to participate in metallic bonding. An example of a trend in reactivity is boron's tendency to form reactive compounds with hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harding.2C_A._Johnson.2C_Janes_2002_113_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harding.2C_A._Johnson.2C_Janes_2002_113-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_reactivity">Chemical reactivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chemical reactivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides">Hydrides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hydrides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most of the elements in the boron group show increasing <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry)" title="Reactivity (chemistry)">reactivity</a> as the elements get heavier in atomic mass and higher in atomic number. <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">Boron</a>, the first element in the group, is generally unreactive with many elements except at high temperatures, although it is capable of forming many compounds with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Boranes" title="Boranes">boranes</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The simplest borane is diborane, or B<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harding.2C_A._Johnson.2C_Janes_2002_113_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harding.2C_A._Johnson.2C_Janes_2002_113-5">[5]</a></sup> Another example is B<sub>10</sub>H<sub>14</sub>.</p>
<p>The next group-13 elements, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, form fewer stable hydrides, although both AlH<sub>3</sub> and GaH<sub>3</sub> exist. Indium, the next element in the group, is not known to form many hydrides, except in complex compounds such as the <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a> complex H<sub>3</sub>InP(Cy)<sub>3</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> No stable compound of thallium and hydrogen has been synthesized in any laboratory.</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides">Oxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Oxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>All of the boron-group elements are known to form a trivalent oxide, with two atoms of the element bonded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent_bonds" title="Covalent bonds">covalently</a> with three atoms of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>. These elements show a trend of increasing <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boron_trioxide" title="Boron trioxide">Boron oxide</a> (B<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) is slightly acidic, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallium(III)_oxide" title="Gallium(III) oxide">gallium oxide</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Ga<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> respectively) are amphoteric, <a href="/wiki/Indium(III)_oxide" title="Indium(III) oxide">indium(III) oxide</a> (In<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) is nearly amphoteric, and <a href="/wiki/Thallium(III)_oxide" title="Thallium(III) oxide">thallium(III) oxide</a> (Tl<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_base" title="Lewis base">Lewis base</a> because it dissolves in acids to form salts. Each of these compounds are stable, but thallium oxide <a href="/wiki/Thermal_decomposition" title="Thermal decomposition">decomposes</a> at temperatures higher than 875 °C.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B2O3powder.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="500" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/B2O3powder.JPG/220px-B2O3powder.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/B2O3powder.JPG/330px-B2O3powder.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/B2O3powder.JPG/440px-B2O3powder.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B2O3powder.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A powdered sample of <a href="/wiki/Boron_trioxide" title="Boron trioxide">boron trioxide</a> (B<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>), one of the oxides of boron</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The elements in group 13 are also capable of forming stable compounds with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halogens" title="Halogens">halogens</a>, usually with the formula MX<sub>3</sub> (where M is a boron-group element and X is a halogen.) The only exception to this is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thallium(III)_iodide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thallium(III) iodide (page does not exist)">thallium(III) iodide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">Fluorine</a>, the first halogen, is able to form stable compounds with every element that has been tested (except <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> and the boron group is no exception. It is even hypothesized that nihonium could form a compound with fluorine, NhF<sub>3</sub>, before spontaneously decaying due to nihonium's radioactivity. <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">Chlorine</a> also forms stable compounds with all of the elements in the boron group, including thallium, and is hypothesized to react with nihonium. All of the elements will react with <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> under the right conditions, as with the other halogens but less vigorously than either chlorine or fluorine. <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">Iodine</a> will react with all natural elements in the periodic table except for the noble gases, and is notable for its explosive reaction with aluminium to form 2AlI<sub>3</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">Astatine</a>, the heaviest halogen, has only formed a few compounds, due to its radioactivity and short half-life, and no reports of a compound with an At–B, –Al, –Ga, –In, –Tl, or –Nh bond have been seen, although scientists think that it should form salts with metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It has been noticed that the elements in the boron group have similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_properties" title="Physical properties">physical properties</a>, although most of boron's are exceptional. For example, all of the elements in the boron group, except for boron itself, are <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">soft</a>. Moreover, all of the other elements in group 13 are relatively reactive at moderate <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperatures</a>, while boron's reactivity only becomes comparable at very high temperatures. One characteristic that all do have in common is having three electrons in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_shell" title="Valence shell">valence shells</a>. Boron, being a metalloid, is a thermal and electrical insulator at room temperature, but a good conductor of heat and electricity at high temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grp.13_elem._chem._8-1"><a href="#cite_note-grp.13_elem._chem.-8">[8]</a></sup> Unlike boron, the metals in the group are good conductors under normal conditions. This is in accordance with the long-standing <a href="/wiki/Generalization" title="Generalization">generalization</a> that all metals conduct heat and electricity better than most non-metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidation_states">Oxidation states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Oxidation states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Inert_pair_effect" title="Inert pair effect">inert s-pair effect</a> is significant in the group-13 elements, especially the heavier ones like thallium. This results in a variety of oxidation states. In the lighter elements, the +3 state is the most stable, but the +1 state becomes more prevalent with increasing atomic number, and is the most stable for thallium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Boron is capable of forming compounds with lower oxidization states, of +1 or +2, and aluminium can do the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Gallium can form compounds with the oxidation states +1, +2 and +3. Indium is like gallium, but its +1 compounds are more stable than those of the lighter elements. The strength of the inert-pair effect is maximal in thallium, which is generally only stable in the oxidation state of +1, although the +3 state is seen in some compounds. Stable and monomeric gallium, indium and thallium radicals with a formal oxidation state of +2 have since been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periodic_trends">Periodic trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Periodic trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are several trends that one could notice as they look at the properties of Boron group members. The Boiling Points of these elements drop from period to period, while densities tend to rise.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear">Nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the exception of the synthetic nihonium, all of the elements of the boron group have stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotopes" title="Isotopes">isotopes</a>. Because all their <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic numbers</a> are odd, boron, gallium and thallium have only two stable isotopes, while aluminium and indium are <a href="/wiki/Monoisotopic_element" title="Monoisotopic element">monoisotopic</a>, having only one. <sup>10</sup>B and <sup>11</sup>B are both stable, as are <sup>27</sup>Al, <sup>69</sup>Ga and <sup>71</sup>Ga, <sup>113</sup>In, and <sup>203</sup>Tl and <sup>205</sup>Tl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> All of these isotopes are readily found in macroscopic quantities in nature. In theory, though, all isotopes with an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> greater than 40 are supposed to be unstable to such decay modes as <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a>. Conversely, all isotopes whose atomic numbers are less than 40 are theoretically supposed to be energetically stable to all forms of decay (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">proton decay</a>, which has never been observed).</p>
<p>Like all other elements, the elements of the boron group have radioactive isotopes, either found in <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">trace</a> quantities in nature or produced <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthetically</a>. The longest-lived of these unstable isotopes is the <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_indium" title="Isotopes of indium">indium isotope</a> <sup>115</sup>In, with its extremely long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of <span class="nowrap">4.41 × 10<sup>14</sup> y</span>. This isotope is relatively important among indium's radioisotopes. The shortest-lived is <sup>7</sup>B, with a half-life of a mere <span class="nowrap">350±50 × 10<sup>−24</sup> s</span>, being the <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_boron" title="Isotopes of boron">boron isotope</a> with the fewest neutrons and a half-life long enough to measure. Some radioisotopes have important roles in scientific research; a few are used in the production of goods for commercial use or, more rarely, as a component of finished products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The boron group has had many names over the years. According to former conventions it was Group IIIB in the European naming system and Group IIIA in the American. The group has also gained two collective names, "earth metals" and "triels". The latter name is derived from the Latin prefix <i>tri-</i> ("three") and refers to the three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_electrons" title="Valence electrons">valence electrons</a> that all of these elements, without exception, have in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_shell" title="Valence shell">valence shells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Kotz.2C_Treichel.2C_R._Townsend_2009_351_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-C._Kotz.2C_Treichel.2C_R._Townsend_2009_351-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boron was known to the ancient Egyptians, but only in the mineral <a href="/wiki/Borax" title="Borax">borax</a>. The metalloid element was not known in its pure form until 1808, when <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> was able to extract it by the method of <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>. Davy devised an experiment in which he dissolved a boron-containing compound in water and sent an electric current through it, causing the elements of the compound to separate into their pure states. To produce larger quantities he shifted from electrolysis to reduction with sodium. Davy named the element <i>boracium</i>. At the same time two French chemists, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacques_Th%C3%A9nard" title="Louis Jacques Thénard">Louis Jacques Thénard</a>, used iron to reduce boric acid. The boron they produced was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidize" title="Oxidize">oxidized</a> to boron oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weeks12_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-weeks12-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aluminium, like boron, was first known in minerals before it was finally extracted from <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>, a common mineral in some areas of the world. <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> and Humphry Davy had each separately tried to extract it. Although neither succeeded, Davy had given the metal its current name. It was only in 1825 that the Danish scientist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a> successfully prepared a rather impure form of the element. Many improvements followed, a significant advance being made just two years later by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a>, whose slightly modified procedure still yielded an impure product. The first pure sample of aluminium is credited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Etienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" title="Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville</a>, who substituted sodium for potassium in the procedure. At that time aluminium was considered precious, and it was displayed next to such metals as gold and silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weeks12_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-weeks12-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> The method used today, <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of aluminium oxide dissolved in cryolite, was developed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martin_Hall" title="Charles Martin Hall">Charles Martin Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_H%C3%A9roult" title="Paul Héroult">Paul Héroult</a> in the late 1880s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weeks12_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-weeks12-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="296" data-file-width="360" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg/220px-Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg/330px-Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphalerite2USGOV.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The mineral zinc blende, more commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a>, in which indium can occur.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Thallium, the heaviest stable element in the boron group, was discovered by <a href="/wiki/William_Crookes" title="William Crookes">William Crookes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claude-Auguste_Lamy" title="Claude-Auguste Lamy">Claude-Auguste Lamy</a> in 1861. Unlike gallium and indium, thallium had not been predicted by <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>, having been discovered before Mendeleev invented the periodic table. As a result, no one was really looking for it until the 1850s when Crookes and Lamy were examining residues from sulfuric acid production. In the <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectra</a> they saw a completely new line, a streak of deep green, which Crookes named after the Greek word θαλλός (<i>thallos</i>), referring to a green shoot or twig. Lamy was able to produce larger amounts of the new metal and determined most of its chemical and physical properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks13_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks13-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indium is the fourth element of the boron group but was discovered before the third, gallium, and after the fifth, thallium. In 1863 <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Reich" title="Ferdinand Reich">Ferdinand Reich</a> and his assistant, <a href="/wiki/Hieronymous_Theodor_Richter" title="Hieronymous Theodor Richter">Hieronymous Theodor Richter</a>, were looking in a sample of the mineral zinc blende, also known as <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> (ZnS), for the spectroscopic lines of the newly discovered element thallium. Reich heated the ore in a coil of <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> metal and observed the lines that appeared in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscope" title="Spectroscope">spectroscope</a>. Instead of the green thallium lines that he expected, he saw a new line of deep indigo-blue. Concluding that it must come from a new element, they named it after the characteristic indigo color it had produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks13_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks13-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_2006_192_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley_2006_192-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gallium minerals were not known before August 1875, when the element itself was discovered. It was one of the elements that the inventor of the periodic table, <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>, had predicted to exist six years earlier. While examining the spectroscopic lines in zinc blende the French chemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Emile_Lecoq_de_Boisbaudran" title="Paul Emile Lecoq de Boisbaudran">Paul Emile Lecoq de Boisbaudran</a> found indications of a new element in the ore. In just three months he was able to produce a sample, which he purified by dissolving it in a <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a> (KOH) solution and sending an electric current through it. The next month he presented his findings to the French Academy of Sciences, naming the new element after the Greek name for Gaul, modern France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks15_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks15-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The last confirmed element in the boron group, nihonium, was not discovered but rather created or synthesized. The element's synthesis was first reported by the Dubna <a href="/wiki/Joint_Institute_for_Nuclear_Research" title="Joint Institute for Nuclear Research">Joint Institute for Nuclear Research</a> team in Russia and the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> in the United States, though it was the Dubna team who successfully conducted the experiment in August 2003. Nihonium was discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moscovium" title="Moscovium">moscovium</a>, which produced a few precious atoms of nihonium. The results were published in January of the following year. Since then around 13 atoms have been synthesized and various isotopes characterized. However, their results did not meet the stringent criteria for being counted as a discovery, and it was the later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RIKEN" title="RIKEN">RIKEN</a> experiments of 2004 aimed at directly synthesizing nihonium that were acknowledged by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a> as the discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The name "boron" comes from the Arabic word for the mineral borax,(بورق, <i>boraq</i>) which was known before boron was ever extracted. The "-on" suffix is thought to have been taken from "carbon"; so the name can regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of "borax" and "carbon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Aluminium was named by Humphry Davy in the early 1800s. It is derived from the Greek word <i>alumen</i>, meaning bitter salt, or the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a></i>, the mineral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Gallium is derived from the Latin <i>Gallia</i>, referring to France, the place of its discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-36">[35]</a></sup> Indium comes from the Latin word <i>indicum</i>, meaning <a href="/wiki/Indigo_dye" title="Indigo dye">indigo dye</a>, and refers to the element's prominent indigo spectroscopic line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Thallium, like indium, is named after the Greek word for the color of its spectroscopic line: <i>thallos</i>, meaning a green twig or shoot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> "Nihonium" is named after <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (<i>Nihon</i> in Japanese), where it was discovered.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_abundance">Occurrence and abundance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Occurrence and abundance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boron">Boron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Boron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Boron, with its atomic number of 5, is a very light element. Almost never found free in nature, it is very low in abundance, composing only 0.001% (10 ppm)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google979_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-google979-40">[39]</a></sup> of the Earth's crust. It is known to occur in over a hundred different minerals and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ores" title="Ores">ores</a>, however: the main source is <a href="/wiki/Borax" title="Borax">borax</a>, but it is also found in <a href="/wiki/Colemanite" title="Colemanite">colemanite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boracite" title="Boracite">boracite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kernite" title="Kernite">kernite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tusionite" title="Tusionite">tusionite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berborite" title="Berborite">berborite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluoborite" title="Fluoborite">fluoborite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Major world miners and extractors of boron include the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>. Turkey is by far the most prominent of these, accounting for around 70% of all boron extraction in the world. The United States is second, most of its yield coming from the state of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aluminium">Aluminium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Aluminium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aluminium, in contrast to boron, is the most abundant metal in the Earth's crust, and the third most abundant element. It composes about 8.2% (82,000 ppm) of the Earth, surpassed only by <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google979_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-google979-40">[39]</a></sup> It is like boron, however, in that it is uncommon in nature as a free element. This is due to aluminium’s tendency to attract oxygen atoms, forming several <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxides</a>. Aluminium is now known to occur in nearly as many minerals as boron, including <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turquoise" title="Turquoise">turquoises</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryls</a>, but the main source is the ore <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a>. The world's leading countries in the extraction of aluminium are <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surinam" title="Surinam">Surinam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallium">Gallium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gallium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gallium is a relatively rare element in the Earth's crust and is not found in as many minerals as its lighter homologues. Its abundance on the Earth is a mere 0.0018% (18 ppm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google979_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-google979-40">[39]</a></sup> Its production is very low compared to other elements, but has increased greatly over the years as extraction methods have improved. Gallium can be found as a trace in a variety of ores, including bauxite and <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a>, and in such minerals as <a href="/wiki/Diaspore" title="Diaspore">diaspore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanite" title="Germanite">germanite</a>. Trace amounts have been found in <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> The gallium content is greater in a few minerals, including <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gallite&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gallite (page does not exist)">gallite</a> (CuGaS<sub>2</sub>), but these are too rare to be counted as major sources and make negligible contributions to the world's supply.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indium">Indium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Indium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Indium is another rare element in the boron group. Even less abundant than gallium at only 0.000005% (0.05 ppm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google979_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-google979-40">[39]</a></sup> it is the 61st most common element in the earth's crust. Very few indium-containing minerals are known, all of them scarce: an example is <a href="/wiki/Indite" title="Indite">indite</a>. Indium is found in several zinc ores, but only in minute quantities; likewise some copper and lead ores contain traces. As is the case for most other elements found in ores and minerals, the indium extraction process has become more efficient in recent years, ultimately leading to larger yields. <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> is the world's leader in indium reserves, but both the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> have comparable amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thallium">Thallium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Thallium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glasfaser_Roving.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1429" data-file-width="773" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Glasfaser_Roving.jpg/170px-Glasfaser_Roving.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Glasfaser_Roving.jpg/255px-Glasfaser_Roving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Glasfaser_Roving.jpg/340px-Glasfaser_Roving.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glasfaser_Roving.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A small bundle of fiberglass</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Thallium is neither rare nor common in the Earth's crust, but falls somewhere in the middle. Its abundance is estimated to be 0.00006% (0.6 ppm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google979_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-google979-40">[39]</a></sup> Thallium is the 56th most common element in the earth's crust, more abundant than indium by a sizeable amount. It is found on the ground in some rocks, in the soil and in clay. Many sulfide ores of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> contain thallium. In minerals it is found in moderate quantities: some examples are <a href="/wiki/Crookesite" title="Crookesite">crookesite</a> (in which it was first discovered), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorandite" title="Lorandite">lorandite</a>, routhierite, bukovite, hutchinsonite and sabatierite. There are other minerals that contain small amounts of thallium, but they are very rare and do not serve as primary sources. <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Macedonia</a> is a notable thallium extractor and producer.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nihonium">Nihonium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nihonium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nihonium is an element that is never found in nature but has been created in a laboratory. It is therefore classified as a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_element" title="Synthetic element">synthetic element</a> with no stable isotopes.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>With the exception of synthetic nihonium, all the elements in the boron group have numerous uses and applications in the production and content of many items.</p>
<p>Boron has found many industrial applications in recent decades, and new ones are still being found. A common application is in <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roesky.2C_Atwood_2003_3.E2.80.9310_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roesky.2C_Atwood_2003_3.E2.80.9310-46">[45]</a></sup> There has been rapid expansion in the market for <a href="/wiki/Borosilicate_glass" title="Borosilicate glass">borosilicate glass</a>; most notable among its special qualities is a much greater resistance to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> than regular glass. Another commercially expanding use of boron and its derivatives is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceramic_materials" title="Ceramic materials">ceramics</a>. Several boron compounds, especially the oxides, have unique and valuable properties that have led to their substitution for other materials that are less useful. Boron may be found in pots, vases, plates, and ceramic pan-handles for its insulating properties. The compound <a href="/wiki/Borax" title="Borax">borax</a> is used in bleaches, for both clothes and teeth. The hardness of boron and some of its compounds give it a wide array of additional uses. A small part (5%) of the boron produced finds use in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roesky.2C_Atwood_2003_3.E2.80.9310_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roesky.2C_Atwood_2003_3.E2.80.9310-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aluminium is a metal with numerous familiar uses in everyday life. It is most often encountered in <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a> materials, in <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electrical</a> devices, especially as the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conductor</a> in cables, and in tools and vessels for cooking and preserving food. Aluminium's lack of reactivity with food products makes it particularly useful for canning. Its high affinity for oxygen makes it a powerful <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a>. Finely powdered pure aluminium oxidizes rapidly in air, generating a huge amount of heat in the process (burning at about <span class="nowrap">5500 °F</span> or <span class="nowrap">3037 °C</span>), leading to applications in <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> and elsewhere that a large amount of heat is needed. Aluminium is a component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alloys" title="Alloys">alloys</a> used for making lightweight bodies for aircraft. Cars also sometimes incorporate aluminium in their framework and body, and there are similar applications in military equipment. Less common uses include components of decorations and some guitars. The element is also sees use in a diverse range of electronics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uv-LED.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Uv-LED.jpg/220px-Uv-LED.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Uv-LED.jpg/330px-Uv-LED.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Uv-LED.jpg/440px-Uv-LED.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uv-LED.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gallium is one of the chief components of blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LEDs</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gallium and its derivatives have only found applications in recent decades. <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">Gallium arsenide</a> has been used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductors" title="Semiconductors">semiconductors</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amplifiers" title="Amplifiers">amplifiers</a>, in solar cells (for example in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellites" title="Satellites">satellites</a>) and in tunnel <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">diodes</a> for FM transmitter circuits. Gallium alloys are used mostly for dental purposes. Gallium ammonium chloride is used for the leads in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transistors" title="Transistors">transistors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> A major application of gallium is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LED</a> lighting. The pure element has been used as a <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopant</a> in semiconductors,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and has additional uses in electronic devices with other elements. Gallium has the property of being able to 'wet' glass and porcelain, and thus can be used to make mirrors and other highly reflective objects. Gallium can be added to alloys of other metals to lower their melting points.</p>
<p>Indium's uses can be divided into four categories: the largest part (70%) of the production is used for coatings, usually combined as <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">indium tin oxide</a> (ITO); a smaller portion (12%) goes into alloys and <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solders</a>; a similar amount is used in electrical components and in semiconductors; and the final 6% goes to minor applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Among the items in which indium may be found are platings, bearings, display devices, heat reflectors, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphors" title="Phosphors">phosphors</a>, and nuclear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Control_rods" title="Control rods">control rods</a>. Indium tin oxide has found a wide range of applications, including glass coatings, <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a>, streetlights, electrophosetic displays (EPDs), electroluminescent displays (ELDs), plasma display panels (PDPs), electrochemic displays (ECs), field emission displays (FEDs), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_lamp" title="Sodium lamp">sodium lamps</a>, windshield glass and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tubes" title="Cathode ray tubes">cathode ray tubes</a>, making it the single most important indium compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thallium is used in its elemental form more often than the other boron-group elements. Uncompounded thallium is used in low-melting glasses, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoelectric_cells" title="Photoelectric cells">photoelectric cells</a>, switches, mercury alloys for low-range glass thermometers, and thallium salts. It can be found in lamps and electronics, and is also used in <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_perfusion_imaging" title="Myocardial perfusion imaging">myocardial imaging</a>. The possibility of using thallium in semiconductors has been researched, and it is a known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> in organic synthesis. <a href="/wiki/Thallium(I)_hydroxide" title="Thallium(I) hydroxide">Thallium hydroxide</a> (TlOH) is used mainly in the production of other thallium compounds. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thallium_sulfate" title="Thallium sulfate">Thallium sulfate</a> (Tl<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) is an outstanding <a href="/wiki/Vermin" title="Vermin">vermin</a>-killer, and it is a principal component in some rat and mouse poisons. However, the United States and some European countries have banned the substance because of its high toxicity to humans. In other countries, though, the market for the substance is growing. Tl<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> is also used in optical systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>None of the group-13 elements has a major biological role in complex animals, but some are at least associated with a living being. As in other groups, the lighter elements usually have more biological roles than the heavier. The heaviest ones are toxic, as are the other elements in the same periods. Boron is essential in most plants, whose cells use it for such purposes as strengthening <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a>. It is found in humans, certainly as a <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace element</a>, but there is ongoing debate over its significance in human nutrition. Boron's chemistry does allow it to form complexes with such important molecules as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>, so it is plausible that it could be of greater use in the human body than previously thought. Boron has also been shown to be able to replace <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> in some of its functions, particularly in the healing of wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Aluminium has no known biological role in plants or animals. Gallium is not essential for the human body, but its relation to iron(III) allows it to become bound to proteins that transport and store iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Gallium can also stimulate metabolism. Indium and its heavier homologues have no biological role, although indium salts in small doses, like gallium, can stimulate metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_2006_192_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley_2006_192-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boron_group&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All of the elements in the boron group can be toxic, given a high enough dose. Some of them are only toxic to plants, some only to animals, and some to both.</p>
<p>As an example of boron toxicity, it has been observed to harm <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> in concentrations exceeding 20 <a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration" title="Molar concentration">mM</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> The symptoms of boron toxicity are numerous in plants, complicating research: they include reduced cell division, decreased shoot and root growth, decreased production of leaf chlorophyll, inhibition of photosynthesis, lowering of stomata conductance, reduced proton extrusion from roots, and deposition of lignin and suborgin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aluminium does not present a prominent toxicity hazard in small quantities, but very large doses are slightly toxic. Gallium is not considered toxic, although it may have some minor effects. Indium is not toxic and can be handled with nearly the same precautions as gallium, but some of its compounds are slightly to moderately toxic.</p>
<p>Thallium, unlike gallium and indium, is extremely toxic, and has caused many poisoning deaths. Its most noticeable effect, apparent even from tiny doses, is <a href="/wiki/Hair_loss" title="Hair loss">hair loss</a> all over the body, but it causes a wide range of other symptoms, disrupting and eventually halting the functions of many organs. The nearly colorless, odorless and tasteless nature of thallium compounds has led to their use by murderers. The incidence of thallium poisoning, intentional and accidental, increased when thallium (with its similarly toxic compound, thallium sulfate) was introduced to control rats and other pests. The use of thallium pesticides has therefore been prohibited since 1975 in many countries, including the USA.</p>
<p>Nihonium is a highly unstable element and decays by emitting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_particles" title="Alpha particles">alpha particles</a>. Due to its strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a>, it would definitely be extremely toxic, although significant quantities of nihonium (larger than a few atoms) have not yet been assembled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup></p>

</div>
